        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'b354ce03-9d0d-44e1-878e-92cc15eeb861'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d6668061" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have received much attention as ideal candidates for next-generation large-scale energy storage systems, but their performance significantly deteriorates at low temperatures, limiting their application in cold or high-altitude environments. This work presents an easier approach to improving low-temperature performance by incorporating nanodiamonds (NDs) into hard carbon anodes derived from cattail grass. The modified anode shows a larger specific surface area, offering more active sites for Na<sup>+</sup>. After 90 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the reversible capacity of the modified anode reaches 365.1&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at room temperature and remains 245.1&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at −40 °C. Even under a high current density of 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, it delivered 108.2&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles with a capacity retention rate of 90%. The improved low-temperature performance is attributed to the introduced&nbsp;NDs in SIBs, which&nbsp;in crease the number of the reversible active sites, reduce charge transfer resistance, lower activation energy, and effectively inhibit the formation of Na dendrites. This work presents a potential pathway into designing efficient and stable anode materials for SIBs at low temperatures.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202410866-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202410866-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have gained significant attention in the field of large-scale energy storage systems due to their low cost, high safety, environmental friendliness, and widespread geographical distribution of sodium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0001_R_d6668053e352" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Rev. (Washington, DC, U. S.) Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nowadays, ensuring the efficient and stable operation of batteries at low temperature (LT) has become a pressing challenge when working at high altitudes or in cold regions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0002_R_d6668053e357" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Anode materials, as a critical component of SIBs, play a crucial role in influencing the kinetics of electrochemical reactions, the diffusion and storage of Na⁺, and the stability of the electrode/electrolyte interface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0003_R_d6668053e362" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Especially at LT, the rapid decrease in the electrochemical performance (including capacity and efficiency) of normal anode materials is one of the key factors leading to the deterioration of battery performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0004_R_d6668053e368" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - SmartMat Interdiscip. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the development of high-capacity, cycle-stable, and efficient anode materials to improve SIB performance under LT conditions is urgently needed.</p>
         
         <p>Current researches on anodes primarily focuses on carbon-based materials, titanium-based materials, alloy-metal materials, and transition-metal compounds materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0005_R_d6668053e376" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater. Nano-Micro Lett. Mater. Today Energy ACS Nano">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among these materials, carbon-based anodes demonstrate minimal volume change during the insertion and extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>, which is advantageous for maintaining stable SIBs operation at LT.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0006_R_d6668053e383" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nano Energy">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This characteristic makes carbon-based materials, particularly hard carbon (HC), the preferred choice for SIB anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0007_R_d6668053e389" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Nano Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Bio-carbon, a type of HC, is favored owing to its abundant raw materials, straightforward synthesis, low cost, and environmental sustainability, rendering it a promising commercial anode material for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0008_R_d6668053e394" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - eScience Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, at LT, bio-carbon anodes face challenges such as capacity decay, driven by many factors like increased charge transfer resistance and higher reaction activation energy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0009_R_d6668053e399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of conventional bio-carbon anodes is generally low due to the irreversible consumption of the electrolyte on the electrode surface. Over the long-term cycling, these anodes fail to provide sufficient reversible active sites to sustain effective output, resulting in capacity degradation and reduced cycle life.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0010_R_d6668053e405" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Storage Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To overcome these limitations, strategies such as selecting the optimal source material and targeted modifications are needed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0011_R_d6668053e410" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Carbon">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>As one of the rapidly reproducing aquatic plants, cattail grass has become a reliable choice for bio-carbon materials due to its abundant resources and low cost, after 500 cycles and its inherent tubular pore structure provides natural storage places for Na⁺, contributing to enhanced capacity in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0012_R_d6668053e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Energy Storage">12</a>, <a href="#smll202410866-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0013_R_d6668053e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Sci. Total Environ.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While nanodiamonds (NDs) have been explored as functional additives in lithium-ion batteries and SIBs anodes for room-temperature (RT) applications, their potential in improving LT performance remains an uncharted territory—a critical gap addressed in this work.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0014_R_d6668053e422" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small Methods Chem. Eng. J. Small Methods J. Energy Storage">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Here, we integrate NDs into cattail grass-derived bio-carbon through a simple synthesis strategy, demonstrating their significant role in enhancing LT performance and long-term cycling stability. The hard carbon with 0.5 wt% NDs (CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub>) exhibits a capacity of 365.1&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 90 cycles at RT and 192.7&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles. At −40 °C, it shows a capacity of 245.1&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 90 cycles, and still maintains a capacity of 108.2&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at a high current density of 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles. The LT performance of CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode is better than many other SIB anode materials (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), providing a feasible scheme for the practical production application. The incorporated NDs play multiple rales in promoting SIBs performance by: i) creating abundant electroactive sites for Na adsorption, ii) modifying the hard carbon structure to enable optimized the insertion/extraction of Na⁺, and iii) catalyzing the formation of a stable and thin solid electrolyte interphase (SEI). This synergistic combination of structural engineering and interfacial optimization significantly enhances Na⁺ storage capabilities, accelerates reaction kinetics, and improves anode durability under LT conditions, advancing the development of practical cryogenic energy storage application.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202410866-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202410866-sec-0020-title">2 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202410866-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202410866-sec-0030-title">2.1 Preparation</h3>
            
            <p>The preparation of the cattail grass-derived HC material is illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202410866-fig-0001">1</a>. First, cattail grass was washed with deionized water and alcohol, respectively, then oven-dried at 80 °C. The dried grass was smashed by a grinder and passed through a 300-mesh sieve. This powder was mixed with commercial detonation NDs of 5–10&nbsp;nm size in a 2&nbsp;M H₂SO₄ solution and stirred for 4 h. Rinsed with deionized water to a neutral pH of 7 and dry at 80 °C for 12 h. Carbonization was carried out at 900 °C for 2 h in an argon atmosphere. The resulting carbonized material was ground for 30 min after cooling. The mass ratios of NDs and cattail grass powder were 0 wt%, 0.25 wt%, and 0.5 wt%, named CG, CG-ND<sub>0.25</sub>, and CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub>, respectively.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202410866-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c974280d-b7aa-402a-b089-224d6e6f5afb/smll202410866-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c974280d-b7aa-402a-b089-224d6e6f5afb/smll202410866-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/51371d75-917b-4051-b704-fc31679b476f/smll202410866-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c974280d-b7aa-402a-b089-224d6e6f5afb/smll202410866-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202410866-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202410866" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematics of the synthesized process of CG-NDs powders.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202410866-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202410866-sec-0040-title">2.2 Material Characterizations</h3>
            
            <p>The crystalline structure, morphology, and element composition of electrode materials were characterized by X-ray diffractometry (XRD, 6000 AS-3K, NOPC), Raman spectroscopy (Witech CRM200, excitation at 532&nbsp;nm wavelength), scanning electron microscopy (SEM, FEIMAGELLAN-400) equipped with an energy-dispersive spectrometer (EDS) profiles, and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEM-2100F, JEOL). The X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo NexsaXPS) was used to analyze the surface chemical states of the samples. The specific surface area was measured by Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET, F-Sorb2400) method.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202410866-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202410866-sec-0050-title">2.3 SIB Cell Preparation and Electrochemical Measurements</h3>
            
            <p>SIB half-cells were assembled with CG or CG-NDs anodes and metallic sodium as counter electrodes. Electrochemical measurements were tested using two electrodes of CR2025 button cells. The working electrodes were prepared by coating slurries containing the 80 wt% active material powders, 10 wt% carbon black, and 10 wt% polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) dissolved in N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) with stirring. The slurry was uniformly coated on a copper (Cu) foil and then dried under vacuum at 120 °C for 12&nbsp;h. The copper foil was cut into a small disc-shaped wafer (12 mm in diameter) and used as the working electrode. The SIBs half-cell uses sodium as the counter electrode, and 1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme as the electrolyte. Glass fiber was used as the separator. The coin-type cells were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox with oxygen content and water content less than 0.1&nbsp;ppm.</p>
            
            <p>A constant current charge/discharge performance was carried out at various rates on a Land Battery Measurement System (CT2001 1A), with a voltage window from 0.01 to 3.0&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> at RT and LT. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was tested at a scanning rate from 0.1 to 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was tested from 0.01&nbsp;V to 3.0&nbsp;V and the frequency from 100&nbsp;kHz to 0.01&nbsp;Hz, respectively, via an electrochemical workstation (CHI 760E).</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202410866-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202410866-sec-0060-title">3 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>XRD and Raman spectroscopy were used to characterize the structure of CG-NDs samples with different NDs content, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202410866-fig-0002">2a,b</a>. The XRD patterns of all samples display two broad peaks at 23° and 43°, corresponding to the (002) and (100) planes of disordered carbon, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0015_R_d6668053e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> There are no diffraction peaks of diamond observed in the spectra of the CG-NDs samples, since the amount of introduced NDs is relatively low. Raman spectroscopy was employed to further study the structural characteristics. The peak observed at 1350 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to the D band, associated with structure defects. And the peak at ≈1590 cm<sup>−1</sup> represents the G band, associated with sp<sup>2</sup> carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0016_R_d6668053e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The degree of defects and disorder of carbon materials can be reflected by the peak intensity ratio (I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub>) of the D band and G band.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0017_R_d6668053e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratios of CG, CG-ND<sub>0.25</sub>, and CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> calculated are of&nbsp;0.946, 0.998, and 1.122, respectively. The increased ratio indicates that the addition of NDs introduced more disordered structures in the formed HC, which is conducive to more adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0018_R_d6668053e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202410866-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9c71c260-a4f2-4494-97f7-d0ee71126e3e/smll202410866-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9c71c260-a4f2-4494-97f7-d0ee71126e3e/smll202410866-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ea9e829a-7681-43a1-8dee-ddaf605b4476/smll202410866-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9c71c260-a4f2-4494-97f7-d0ee71126e3e/smll202410866-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202410866-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202410866" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural characterization of CG, CG-ND<sub>0.25</sub>, and CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> powders. a) XRD pattern. b) Raman spectra. c) Nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherms. d–f) Pore size distribution of CG, CG-ND<sub>0.25</sub>, and CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> powders.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The information of the specific surface area and pore size distribution is also crucial for the design of high performance anode materials. The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption isothermal curves of CG, CG-ND<sub>0.25</sub>, and CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202410866-fig-0002">2c</a>. The addition of NDs significantly increases the specific surface area of the sample from 73.2 (CG) to 311.2 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> (CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub>), which is related to the NDs enhanced pores structure of HC and the additional intrinsic high intrinsic specific surface area of NDs (277.4 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The increased specific surface area indicates that the material offers more active sites for Na⁺ storage, which is beneficial for enhancing electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0019_R_d6668053e633" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nano Energy">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The pore size distribution, estimated using the Density Functional Theory (DFT) model, is presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202410866-fig-0002">2d–f</a>. The pore widths of CG, CG-ND<sub>0.25</sub>, and CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> are mainly within 2&nbsp;nm, indicating that all the materials are composed of microporous. The addition of NDs leads to an increase in pore width, which can be attributed to their influence on modulating the degree of graphitization during the high-temperature carbonization process. This modulation helps maintain a more disordered carbon framework, resulting in larger pore widths in CG–NDs compared to CG. Moreover, the larger pore width is conducive to the full contact of the electrode and electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0020_R_d6668053e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Carbon Energy eScience">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The morphology and structure of carbon materials was further characterized using SEM and TEM. CG samples show a natural tubular pore structure with particle sizes between 20 and 30&nbsp;µm (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202410866-fig-0003">3a,b</a>). The typical disordered microstructure was observed by HRTEM with the interlayer spacing of about 0.393&nbsp;nm, which is larger than the graphite lattice spacing of 0.335&nbsp;nm, facilitating Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202410866-fig-0003">3c</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0021_R_d6668053e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After introducing NDs, the CG-ND<sub>0.25</sub> and CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> materials remained tubular pore structure with similar size (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202410866-fig-0003">3d,g</a>). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202410866-fig-0003">3e,f,h,i</a>, NDs are effectively embedded in the CG matrix. These stable NDs in the CG matrix keep their original features with an average size of 5–10&nbsp;nm and interlayer spacing of 0.206&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), corresponding to the diamond (111) plane.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0022_R_d6668053e685" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Energy Storage">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Noticeably, during the carbonization process at 900 °C in argon ambient, the inorganic NDs with high thermal stability have high reducing activity, which will accelerate the local non-graphitized carbonization of bio-carbon precursor near the ND particles, promoting the formation of a more disordered carbon framework, as observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202410866-fig-0003">3f,i</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0023_R_d6668053e693" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Nanotechnol. Chin. Phys. Lett.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The energy dispersive spectrometry (EDS) images of CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> powder show that the sample is mainly composed of C and O elements (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202410866-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/979b02e6-4d14-4caf-bfd4-6b975342accf/smll202410866-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/979b02e6-4d14-4caf-bfd4-6b975342accf/smll202410866-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/80ae597a-6c13-4a0b-86f8-6a896ef3875b/smll202410866-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/979b02e6-4d14-4caf-bfd4-6b975342accf/smll202410866-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202410866-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202410866" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM, TEM and HRTEM images of a–c) CG, d–f) CG-ND<sub>0.25</sub>, and g–i) CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> powders.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The chemical compositions of CG and CG-NDs powders were further analyzed using XPS. The measured spectra of all the samples confirm the presence of C and O elements (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a</a>, Supporting Information). In the C 1s spectra of CG, CG-ND<sub>0.25,</sub> and CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4b–d</a>, Supporting Information), three characteristic peaks are observed centered at 284.6, 285.3, and 286.4&nbsp;eV, corresponding to the typical characteristics of sp<sup>2</sup>, sp<sup>3</sup>, and C─O bonds, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0024_R_d6668053e747" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Alloys Compd. Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information increasing the ND content significantly raises the sp<sup>3</sup> (C─C) bond ratio.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical properties of the samples were revealed by testing the assembled HC||Na half-cells. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202410866-fig-0004">4a</a> shows the first galvanostatic charge and discharge (GCD) curves at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> for assessing the Na storage capacity. The curves exhibit two potential regions, named the slope region above 0.1&nbsp;V and the platform region below 0.1&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0025_R_d6668053e768" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. Eng. J.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode has the best Na storage performance with a discharge/charge capacity of 547.1/366.5&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, accompanied by an ICE of 66.9%. CG-ND<sub>0.25</sub> anode shows a slight decrease in performance with an ICE of 59.3%. In contrast, the discharge/charge capacities of CG are 499.9/229.4&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, with a lower ICE of 45.8%. During the first cycle, a large amount of irreversible Na<sup>+</sup> is consumed by the decomposition of the electrolyte and the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on the anode surface, resulting in a low ICE of the anode material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0026_R_d6668053e785" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nano Energy">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By comparing the TEM images of the post-cycling electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), it is clearly observed that the SEI of CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> is thinner and more homogeneous than that of&nbsp;CG. This structural improvement allows CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode better to withstand the (de)insertion of Na, helping for enhancing the cyclic stability of SIBs. Additionally, incorporating NDs introduces more reversible active sites, reducing the irreversible consumption of Na⁺ during the charge/discharge process, and contributing to the higher ICE of CG-NDs anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0027_R_d6668053e797" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Power Sources">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202410866-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ca6fe7fe-86f3-4379-8001-24c7702d551e/smll202410866-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ca6fe7fe-86f3-4379-8001-24c7702d551e/smll202410866-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8a27dcc6-f506-4c19-9fcb-0a85f2bed9a8/smll202410866-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ca6fe7fe-86f3-4379-8001-24c7702d551e/smll202410866-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202410866-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202410866" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of half-cells with CG and CG-NDs anodes&nbsp;at RT (25 °C). a) The initial galvanostatic charge and discharge curves at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and b) cycle performance of 90 cycles. c) Rate performance of different current densities. d) Cycle performance of 500 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Nyquist plot after 90 cycles (illustration of fitted equivalent circuit). f) CV curves for CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode at 0.1–0.8&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. g) A logarithmic linear relationship between current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>). h) The capacitive contribution ratio of CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode at different scan rates.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202410866-fig-0004">4b,d</a>, the cycle performances of the samples were tested at 0.1 and 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Among them, CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode exhibits the highest reversible capacity (365.1&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 90 cycles) and the best cycle stability (192.7&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202410866-fig-0004">4c</a> compares the rate capabilities of the samples at different current densities from 0.1 to 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. As current density increases, all cells exhibit capacity decay, which becomes more pronounced at higher current densities. CG anode displays capacities of 221.9, 209.8, 191.9, 173.9, and 151.0&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Under the same conditions, CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode achieves higher capacities of 344.8, 314.6, 290.9, 266.6, and 230.4&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. When the current density is returned to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, all the samples show high reversibility.</p>
         
         <p>Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) results for the samples are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202410866-fig-0004">4e</a> (the equivalent circuit is shown in the inset). In the Nyquist plots, each sample shows a semicircular shape in the middle-frequency regions, corresponding to charge transfer resistance (Rct) at the electrode/electrolyte interface. A linear segment in the low-frequency region represents the Warburg impedance associated with Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0028_R_d6668053e876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nano Energy">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the corresponding fitting data, CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> exhibits a lower Rct (22.1 Ω) than that for CG (47.7 Ω), suggesting that the addition of NDs enhances the reaction kinetics that facilitates electron and ion transfer at the electrode interface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0018_R_d6668053e884" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The enhancement in charge transfer kinetics well explains the superior rate performance observed in CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">To further investigate the electrochemical reactions kinetics of CG and CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub>, cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were performed at scan rates ranging from 0.1 to 0.8&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a>, Supporting Information; Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202410866-fig-0004">4f</a>). Both CG and CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anodes exhibit typical HC redox peaks near 0–0.1&nbsp;V, and the peak intensity increased with the rising scan rate. The area enclosed in CV curves corresponds to the total charge storage, which includes both diffusion- and capacitive-controlled processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0029_R_d6668053e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Environ. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According that the current (<i>i</i>) obeys a power-law relationship with the scan rate (<i>v</i>), the behavior of the peak current is correlated using equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202410866-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>):
            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202410866-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/9ff6a09b-4d1d-4874-a0ce-e8ba7ea2102b/smll202410866-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202410866:smll202410866-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/smll202410866-math-0001.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mi>a</mi>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mi>b</mi>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i = a{v^b}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where, <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are the fitting parameters, the <i>b</i>-value is given by the slope of the log<i>i</i> versus log<i>v</i> plots.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0030_R_d6668053e947" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. Eng. J.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally, the <i>b</i>-value close to 0.5 indicates a diffusion-controlled process, while <i>b</i>-value near 1.0 indicates a capacitive-controlled process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0031_R_d6668053e956" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The <i>b</i>-values of CG anode are 0.561 and 0.678 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the Na storage in the platform region is primarily diffusion-controlled. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202410866-fig-0004">4g</a> shows that the <i>b</i>-values of CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode are 0.706 and 0.801, which means that the Na stored process of CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode in the platform area is the joint action of diffusion-controlled process and capacitive-controlled process.</div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The contributions of diffusion-controlled and capacitive-controlled processes in the samples are further analyzed using equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202410866-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>):

            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202410866-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/31e5cee7-cff0-44ef-8139-a41b37e3ca77/smll202410866-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202410866:smll202410866-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/smll202410866-math-0002.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                           <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                           <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                              <mi>V</mi>
                           </mfenced>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>k</mi>
                              <mn>1</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <mi>v</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>k</mi>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                                 <mo>/</mo>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </mrow>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i\;\left( V \right) = {k_1}v + {k_2}{v^{1/2}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div><i>v</i>&nbsp;is the scan rate and <i>i</i> represents the total current response at a fixed potential (V). This current can be decomposed into two terms, <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>, representing capacitive effects and diffusion-controlled contributions, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0032_R_d6668053e1026" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nano Energy">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The contribution ratios of capacitive and diffusion-controlled processes for CG and CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anodes at different scan rates are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6c</a>, Supporting Information, and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202410866-fig-0004">4h</a>. As the scan rate increases, the capacitive contribution for both samples rises. At the scan rate of 0.8&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the capacitive contribution for CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode reaches 78%, much higher than that of CG anode (60%). This enhanced capacitive contribution is attributed to the increased specific surface area derived from adding NDs. The CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> electrode effectively provides more active sites for Na<sup>+</sup>, shortening the ion transport path within the material, mitigating the effect of the slower solid-state ion diffusion process, and obtaining better electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0033_R_d6668053e1049" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Engl. ACS Nano">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></div>
         
         <p>The LT electrochemical properties of the half-cells with various anodes were tested at −40 °C. CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode exhibits impressive Na storage capability and excellent capacity retention rates at LT. At 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> current density, CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode achieves an initial charge capacity of 270.3&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with an ICE of 62.9% (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202410866-fig-0005">5a</a>). After 90 cycles, the reversible capacity remains at 245.1&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, much higher than the 157.7&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> from CG anode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202410866-fig-0005">5b</a>). This performance is superior to the LT capabilities of many reported anodes for SIBs (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202410866-fig-0005">5c</a>, the CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode delivers specific capacities of 273.5, 246.1, 196.5, 107.0, and 46.5&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities ranging from 0.1 to 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, in turn. In comparison, CG anode achieves capacities of 148.9, 119.9, 67.1, 40.0, and 20.9&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at the same rates. After activation with 10 cycles at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, we tested the constant current discharge/charge performance at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202410866-fig-0005">5d</a>). Remarkably, after 500 cycles, the CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode retained a reversible capacity of 108.2&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention rate of 90%. It has been experimentally and theoretically demonstrated that NDs are easy to absorb Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0024_R_d6668053e1107" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Alloys Compd. Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a>, <a href="#smll202410866-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0029_R_d6668053e1107" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Environ. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, during the charge and discharge process, NDs play a crucial role in increasing capacity, which has been confirmed in the RT environment, and it is also applicable at LT. Moreover, by introducing NDs in the anode, the super-hard diamond has good mechanical strength, which has a strong supporting effect on the micro-nano structure of the porous anode and its ND-rich SEI layer for ensuring the stability of the anode structure for&nbsp;long-term&nbsp;cycles. The increase in the specific surface area of the porous CG-NDs anode provides more reversible reaction sites for the storage of Na<sup>+</sup> and improves the permeability of the electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0034_R_d6668053e1115" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202410866-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/65a367d4-05fa-4434-b4e4-c8f230ade6fa/smll202410866-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/65a367d4-05fa-4434-b4e4-c8f230ade6fa/smll202410866-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4cbdb7c5-a958-44c1-b26b-24ff20bda124/smll202410866-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/65a367d4-05fa-4434-b4e4-c8f230ade6fa/smll202410866-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202410866-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202410866" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of half-cells with CG and CG-NDs anodes at LT (−40 °C). a) The initial galvanostatic charge and discharge curves at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and b) cycle performance of 90 cycles. c) Rate performance of different current densities, and d) cycle performance of 500 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Arrhenius curves and the calculated activation energies for Rct as obtained from the Nyquist plots of the CG and CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> electrodes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Notably, when investigating the LT capacity performance of anodes with higher NDs concentrations (e.g., CG-ND<sub>1.0</sub>, CG-ND<sub>5.0</sub>, and CG-ND<sub>10.0</sub>). It is found that the results are unsatisfactory (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a,b</a>, Supporting Information). The results show that a small amount of NDs (e.g., CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub>) can significantly improve the LT performance of the anode materials, but the performance improvement diminishes with excessive ND addition (e.g., CG-ND<sub>1.0</sub>). This is likely due to agglomeration of the excess NDs, resulting in uneven dispersion and a reduced effective surface area for Na⁺ absorption.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0024_R_d6668053e1162" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Alloys Compd. Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The slow Na<sup>+</sup> desorption process is considered to be a major obstacle to the LT performance of SIBs, which is closely related to the activation energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) of the reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0035_R_d6668053e1174" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Carbon Energy">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To estimate the <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> during the Na<sup>+</sup> (dis)charge process, EIS measurements were carried out from 25 to −40 °C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). The <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> can be deduced from Nyquist plots based on the Arrhenius equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202410866-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a>):
            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202410866-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b6c6654f-7948-4c75-bf3b-311122a23497/smll202410866-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202410866:smll202410866-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/smll202410866-math-0003.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mfrac>
                              <mn>1</mn>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi>R</mi>
                                 <mi>c</mi>
                                 <mi>t</mi>
                              </mrow>
                           </mfrac>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mi>A</mi>
                           <mi>e</mi>
                           <mi>x</mi>
                           <mi>p</mi>
                           <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                              <mrow>
                                 <mo linebreak="badbreak">−</mo>
                                 <mfrac>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mi>a</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mi>R</mi>
                                       <mi>T</mi>
                                    </mrow>
                                 </mfrac>
                              </mrow>
                           </mfenced>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}\frac{1}{{Rct}} = Aexp\left( { - \frac{{{E_a}}}{{RT}}} \right)\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>where <i>A</i> is the pre-exponential factor, <i>T</i> is the absolute temperature, and <i>R</i> is the Boltzmann constant.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0036_R_d6668053e1233" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Chem. Eng. J.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The results show that the <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> for Na<sup>+</sup> migration in CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> (118.6 meV) is lower than that in CG (139.4 meV) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202410866-fig-0005">5e</a>). This reveals that the incorporation of NDs effectively reduces <i>E</i><sub>a</sub>, helping to mitigate the slow Na<sup>+</sup> desorption issue at LT.</div>
         
         <p>To further investigate the Na storage mechanism of CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> under LT conditions, ex-situ XRD tests were conducted. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202410866-fig-0006">6a</a> presents the XRD patterns of the CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode during the first discharge/charge. As the voltage decreases from 1.0&nbsp;V to 0&nbsp;V, one of the major diffraction peaks (002) of the hard carbon gradually shifts to a lower angle, reaching a maximum shift of 0.74°. It indicates that the lattice expansion occurs&nbsp;slightly with the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>. Upon subsequent charging to 3.0&nbsp;V, the Na⁺ desorption process is completed, and the (002) peak position was essentially returns to its original state. These findings further confirm the high structural stability and electrochemical reversibility of the CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode, which contribute to its superior capacity retention and long-term cycling stability under LT conditions.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202410866-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/328ed510-e80f-4426-837f-bbdc77bccb01/smll202410866-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/328ed510-e80f-4426-837f-bbdc77bccb01/smll202410866-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/41813938-2ab5-4af2-bccb-cfc529f65b7f/smll202410866-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/328ed510-e80f-4426-837f-bbdc77bccb01/smll202410866-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202410866-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202410866" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Ex-situ XRD during the initial cycle of CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub>. SEM and EDS images of b–h) CG and b′–g′) CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anodes after 90 cycles and 1 month of exposure to air.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>SEM and EDS tests were performed on CG and CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> anode electrodes after 90 cycles and 1 month of air exposure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202410866-fig-0006">6b–h′</a>). Compared to the CG anode before cycling (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information), numerous needle-like substances appeared on the cycled CG anode, with concentrated Na and O elements. This phenomenon may be attributed to the appearance of Na dendrites on the surface of the electrode during the cycling process. When they are exposed to air, spiny Na<sub>2</sub>O gradually forms. In contrast, the CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> electrode maintained its original form without needle-like substance, and EDS images revealed a uniform distribution of Na, F, and P elements. These findings suggest that the addition of NDs effectively inhibits the formation of Na dendrites and induces the uniform deposition of Na on the anode surface, which enhances the long-term stability of LT SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202410866-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202410866-bib-0037_R_d6668053e1314" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nat. Commun. Joule Adv. Energy Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202410866-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202410866-sec-0070-title">4 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we design a new bio-carbon anode material for SIBs prepared by cattail grass and NDs. The optimized&nbsp;CG-ND<sub>0.5</sub> exhibits high reversible capacity and long cycle stability at −40 °C, superior to the various traditional anodes. The NDs can effectively modify&nbsp;the overall structure of the anode, provid more reversible active sites for Na<sup>+</sup>, form a thin and homogeneous SEI, reduced charge transfer resistance and activation energy, enhance reaction kinetics, and facilitae efficient electron and ion transport at the electrode interface. Additionally, NDs inhibit Na dendrite formation, supporting long-term cycling stability at LT.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
